# Experiment Results Summary: Neural Network Architectures for Gene Expression Prediction

Based on the experimental results, I can provide an overview of the conducted experiments and their outcomes for optimizing the feedforward neural network architecture to minimize loss/RMSE.

## Control Group Results
The baseline model with standard configuration achieved:
- Final RMSE: 0.082716 (after 390 epochs)
- Configuration: 4 layers of 256 units each
- Activation: LeakyReLU(0.1) for hidden layers, ELU(alpha=0.01, beta=0.01) for output
- Optimizer: Adam with default learning rate
- No regularization

## Experimental Group Results
Multiple architectures were tested with the following results:

1. **AdamW Optimizer Variation**:
   - RMSE: 0.076-0.080 (final epochs)
   - Configuration: [256, 128] hidden layers
   - Activation: ReLU
   - Optimizer: AdamW with learning rate 0.001 and weight decay 0.01
   - Training achieved best results around epoch 130 with RMSE of 0.0735

2. **Wider Network**: 
   - Architecture: [512, 512] hidden layers
   - ReLU activation
   
3. **Network with Dropout**:
   - Architecture: [256, 128] hidden layers
   - 20% dropout rate
   
4. **Deeper Network**:
   - Architecture: [256, 128, 64] hidden layers
   
5. **Batch Normalization**:
   - Architecture: [256, 128] hidden layers
   - Batch normalization between layers

From the experiments, the most effective architecture appears to be the AdamW optimizer variation, which reached an RMSE of approximately 0.0735, significantly better than the baseline model's 0.0827.

The AdamW optimization with weight decay of 0.01 provided regularization benefits while maintaining good convergence properties. The smaller architecture [256, 128] was also more efficient than the baseline's four layers of 256 units each.

This suggests that a more compact network architecture with appropriate regularization through weight decay and a modern optimizer (AdamW) is more effective for this gene expression prediction task than the baseline model or other variations tested.
# Analysis of Neural Network Optimization Experiment

## Overview of Experiment
The experiment tested a neural network model for predicting high-resolution gene expression using low-resolution spatial transcriptomics data and histological features. A control model was established with promising results, and various experimental modifications were planned to optimize performance.

## Control Model Performance
- **Architecture**: 4 layers x 256 units with LeakyReLU activation
- **Learning rate**: 0.0001
- **Batch size**: 27
- **Optimizer**: Adam (default parameters)
- **Performance**: Final RMSE of approximately 0.0757-0.0795
- **Training pattern**: Rapid initial improvement followed by gradual convergence

## Key Observations

1. **Baseline Effectiveness**: The control model demonstrated strong predictive capability, with RMSE decreasing from ~0.197 initially to ~0.076-0.080 after 400 epochs.

2. **Convergence Pattern**: Training showed classic learning dynamics with:
   - Fast initial improvement (first 50 epochs)
   - Slower but steady progress in middle epochs
   - Minor fluctuations between RMSE 0.07-0.09 in later stages
   - Best performance reaching ~0.069 RMSE at optimal points

3. **Training Efficiency**: Each epoch processed 17 batches at 113-152 iterations per second, with total training time around 52-54 seconds.

## Planned Experimental Variations

The experiment designed two sets of modifications to potentially improve the baseline:

### Architecture and Training Dynamics
- Smaller learning rate (0.00005)
- Larger network capacity (4x384)
- Deeper network (5x256)
- Smaller batch size (16)
- Modified Adam beta parameters

### Regularization and Activation Variants
- Adjusted LeakyReLU slope (0.05)
- Residual connections
- Minimal dropout (0.05)
- Weight decay (0.0001)
- ELU activation instead of LeakyReLU

## Conclusions

1. The control model established a strong baseline for the spatial transcriptomics prediction task with consistent convergence.

2. The experiment setup appears methodical, testing isolated changes to network architecture, optimization parameters, and regularization techniques.

3. The results suggest the baseline architecture is already effective, with RMSE values indicating good predictive performance, though there may be room for further optimization.

4. The planned variations address key aspects of neural network design and could potentially yield incremental improvements in prediction accuracy.
